### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, let's understand the problem:
- We are given a positive integer `n > 0` (`0 < n`).
- The logarithmic condition is `log₂(log₁₆(n)) = log₄(log₄(n))`.
- We need to prove that the sum of the digits of `n` in base 10 is 13.

However, the logarithmic condition is not well-defined for arbitrary `n > 0` because:
1. The logarithm `log₁₆(n)` is only defined when `n > 0`, which is satisfied here (`n > 0`).
2. The logarithm `log₂(log₁₆(n))` is only defined when `log₁₆(n) > 0`, i.e., when `n > 1` (since `log₁₆(n) > 0` iff `n > 1` because `log₁₆(1) = 0` and `log₁₆` is increasing).
3. Similarly, `log₄(log₄(n))` is only defined when `log₄(n) > 0`, i.e., when `n > 1` (since `log₄(1) = 0` and `log₄` is increasing).

But the Lean 4 statement does not include the condition `n > 1` explicitly. This means that if `n = 1`, the expression `Real.logb 16 n` is `Real.logb 16 1 = 0`, and `Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = Real.logb 2 0` is undefined (`Real.logb 2 0` is `0 / Real.log 2 = 0` in Lean 4, but mathematically it is undefined because `log 0` is undefined). However, Lean 4 defines `Real.logb x y = Real.log y / Real.log x` and `Real.log 0 = 0`, so `Real.logb 2 0 = 0 / Real.log 2 = 0`. Similarly, `Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n)` is `Real.logb 4 0 = 0` when `n = 1`. So the condition becomes `0 = 0`, which is true. However, the sum of the digits of `n = 1` is `1`, not `13`. 

But the problem statement says `0 < n` (`n ≥ 1`), and the condition holds for `n = 1`, but the conclusion is false. This suggests that either:
1. The problem statement is missing the condition `n > 1`, or
2. The problem statement is incorrect as stated.

Looking at the Lean 4 statement, it seems that the condition is `0 < n`, and the conclusion is `(Nat.digits 10 n).sum = 13`. But for `n = 1`, `(Nat.digits 10 1).sum = 1 ≠ 13`, so the statement is false as written. 

However, the logarithmic condition is `Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n)`. Let's check what this implies for `n > 1`.

First, recall that `Real.logb b x = Real.log x / Real.log b`. So:
1. `Real.logb 16 n = Real.log n / Real.log 16 = Real.log n / (4 * Real.log 2)` because `Real.log 16 = Real.log (2⁴) = 4 * Real.log 2`.
2. `Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = Real.log (Real.logb 16 n) / Real.log 2 = Real.log (Real.log n / (4 * Real.log 2)) / Real.log 2`.
3. `Real.logb 4 n = Real.log n / Real.log 4 = Real.log n / (2 * Real.log 2)` because `Real.log 4 = Real.log (2²) = 2 * Real.log 2`.
4. `Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n) = Real.log (Real.logb 4 n) / Real.log 4 = Real.log (Real.log n / (2 * Real.log 2)) / (2 * Real.log 2)`.

The condition becomes:
`Real.log (Real.log n / (4 * Real.log 2)) / Real.log 2 = Real.log (Real.log n / (2 * Real.log 2)) / (2 * Real.log 2)`.

Multiply both sides by `2 * Real.log 2`:
`2 * Real.log (Real.log n / (4 * Real.log 2)) = Real.log (Real.log n / (2 * Real.log 2))`.

Let `L = Real.log n` and `C = Real.log 2`. Then the equation is:
`2 * Real.log (L / (4 * C)) = Real.log (L / (2 * C))`.

Simplify the logarithms:
`2 * (Real.log L - Real.log (4 * C)) = Real.log L - Real.log (2 * C)`.

Further simplify:
`2 * (Real.log L - (Real.log 4 + Real.log C)) = Real.log L - (Real.log 2 + Real.log C)`.

But `Real.log 4 = 2 * Real.log 2 = 2 * C`, so:
`2 * (Real.log L - (2 * C + C)) = Real.log L - (C + C)` →
`2 * (Real.log L - 3 * C) = Real.log L - 2 * C` →
`2 * Real.log L - 6 * C = Real.log L - 2 * C` →
`Real.log L = 4 * C` →
`Real.log L = Real.log (2⁴) = Real.log 16` →
`L = 16` →
`Real.log n = 16` →
`n = e¹⁶`.

But `n` is a positive integer, and `e¹⁶` is not an integer (`e ≈ 2.718`, `e¹⁶ ≈ 8.886 * 10⁶`). So there are no positive integers `n` satisfying the given logarithmic condition.

But Lean 4 defines `Real.logb x y = Real.log y / Real.log x` and `Real.log 0 = 0`, so `Real.logb x 0 = 0` for any `x > 0`, `x ≠ 1`. Therefore:
1. If `n = 1`, then `Real.logb 16 n = 0` and `Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = 0`. Similarly, `Real.logb 4 n = 0` and `Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n) = 0`. So the condition holds (`0 = 0`), but the sum of digits is `1 ≠ 13`.
2. If `n > 1`, then `Real.log n > 0`, and the condition simplifies to `n = e¹⁶`, but `n` must be an integer, and `e¹⁶` is not an integer, so no solutions exist.

However, the Lean 4 statement includes `n = 1` as a possibility, and the condition holds for `n = 1`, but the conclusion is false. Therefore, the theorem as stated is incorrect because it claims that the sum of digits is `13` for all `n > 0` satisfying the logarithmic condition, but it fails for `n = 1`.

But the problem is from AMC 12A 2020 Problem 10, where the correct statement is:
\"Let `n` be a positive integer such that `log₂(log₁₆(n)) = log₄(log₄(n))`. What is the sum of the digits of `n` in base 10?\"

The correct solution is that `n` must be `e¹⁶`, but since `n` is an integer, there is no solution. However, the problem is phrased in a way that suggests that `n` is a positive integer satisfying the condition, and the sum of its digits is `13`. But no such `n` exists, so the problem is vacuously true, but Lean 4's statement is not vacuously true because `n = 1` is a counterexample to the conclusion.

But looking at the Lean 4 statement again, it says:
```lean4
theorem amc12a_2020_p10
(n : ℕ)
(h₀ : 0 < n)
(h₁ : Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n)) :
(Nat.digits 10 n).sum = 13 := sorry
```
The hypothesis `h₁` is not actually `log₂(log₁₆(n)) = log₄(log₄(n))` in the mathematical sense, because Lean 4 defines `Real.logb x y` as `Real.log y / Real.log x` and `Real.log 0 = 0`, so `Real.logb x 0 = 0`. 

But in the problem, `log₁₆(n)` is `log n / log 16`, and `log₂(log₁₆(n))` is `log(log n / log 16) / log 2`, but Lean 4 would interpret `Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n)` as `Real.log (Real.logb 16 n) / Real.log 2`, which is `Real.log (Real.log n / Real.log 16) / Real.log 2`, and similarly for the other side. So the Lean 4 statement is actually correct in the sense that it matches the mathematical statement, except that `Real.logb x y` is defined even when `y ≤ 0` (`Real.log y = 0` if `y = 0` and `Real.log y` is undefined if `y < 0`). 

However, in the problem, `log₁₆(n)` is only defined when `n > 0`, and `log₂(log₁₆(n))` is defined when `log₁₆(n) > 0`, i.e., `n > 1`. But in Lean 4, `Real.logb 16 n = 0` when `n = 1`, and `Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = 0`, and similarly `Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n) = 0` when `n = 1`, so the condition holds for `n = 1`. 

But the sum of the digits of `n = 1` is `1`, not `13`. So the Lean 4 statement is false as written, because `n = 1` satisfies the hypotheses but not the conclusion.

But perhaps the original problem intended `n` to be an integer greater than `1`. In that case, we can proceed as follows:

Assume `n > 1`. Then `log₁₆(n) > 0` and `log₄(n) > 0`. The condition is:
`log₂(log₁₆(n)) = log₄(log₄(n))`.

Let `L = log n` and `C = log 2`. Then `log₁₆(n) = L / (4 C)` and `log₄(n) = L / (2 C)`. The condition becomes:
`log₂(L / (4 C)) = log₄(L / (2 C))`.

But `log₂(L / (4 C)) = (log(L / (4 C))) / log 2 = (log L - log(4 C)) / C = (log L - (2 C + C)) / C = (log L - 3 C) / C = (log L)/C - 3`.

Similarly, `log₄(L / (2 C)) = (log(L / (2 C))) / log 4 = (log L - log(2 C)) / (2 C) = (log L - (C + C)) / (2 C) = (log L - 2 C) / (2 C) = (log L)/(2 C) - 1`.

So the condition is:
`(log L)/C - 3 = (log L)/(2 C) - 1`.

Multiply by `2 C`:
`2 (log L) - 6 C = (log L) - 2 C` →
`(log L) = 4 C` →
`log L = log 16` →
`L = 16` →
`log n = 16` →
`n = e¹⁶`.

But `n` is an integer, and `e¹⁶` is not an integer, so no such `n` exists. 

But the Lean 4 statement is not vacuously true because `n = 1` is a counterexample to the conclusion, and it satisfies the hypotheses. 

However, the original AMC problem likely intended `n` to be an integer greater than `1`, and the sum of its digits is `13` under some other condition. 

But the Lean 4 statement is as given, and it is false because `n = 1` is a counterexample.

theorem imo_1960_p2
(x : ℝ)
(h₀ : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x)
(h₁ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x))^2 ≠ 0)
(h₂ : (4 * x^2) / (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2*x))^2 < 2*x + 9) :
-(1 / 2) ≤ x ∧ x < 45 / 8 :=
  have h₃ : -(1 / 2 : ℝ) ≤ x := by
    have h₃₁ : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x := h₀
    have h₃₂ : -(1 / 2 : ℝ) ≤ x := by
      linarith
    exact h₃₂
  
  have h₄ : x < (45 / 8 : ℝ) := by
    have h₄₁ : 0 ≤ Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) := Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x)
    have h₄₂ : Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) ≠ 1 := by
      by_contra h
      have h₄₃ : Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) = 1 := by linarith
      have h₄₄ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 = 0 := by
        rw [h₄₃]
        norm_num
      have h₄₅ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 ≠ 0 := h₁
      contradiction
    have h₄₃ : 0 < (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 := by
      by_contra h
      have h₄₄ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 ≤ 0 := by linarith
      have h₄₅ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 = 0 := by
        nlinarith [Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x), Real.sq_sqrt (by linarith : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x)]
      have h₄₆ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 ≠ 0 := h₁
      contradiction
    have h₄₄ : 0 < 1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) ∨ 1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) < 0 := by
      by_cases h₄₅ : 0 < 1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)
      · exact Or.inl h₄₅
      · have h₄₆ : 1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) ≤ 0 := by linarith
        have h₄₇ : 1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) ≠ 0 := by
          intro h₄₈
          have h₄₉ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 = 0 := by
            rw [h₄₈]
            norm_num
          have h₅₀ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 ≠ 0 := h₁
          contradiction
        have h₄₈ : 1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) < 0 := by
          cases' lt_or_gt_of_ne h₄₇ with h₄₉ h₄₉
          · linarith
          · exfalso
            linarith
        exact Or.inr h₄₈
    have h₄₅ : 0 < (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 := by positivity
    have h₄₆ : (4 * x ^ 2) / (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 < 2 * x + 9 := h₂
    have h₄₇ : 4 * x ^ 2 < (2 * x + 9) * (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 := by
      have h₄₈ : 0 < (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 := by positivity
      have h₄₉ : (4 * x ^ 2) / (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 < 2 * x + 9 := h₂
      have h₅₀ : (4 * x ^ 2) / (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 * (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 < (2 * x + 9) * (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 := by
        gcongr
      have h₅₁ : (4 * x ^ 2) / (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 * (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 = 4 * x ^ 2 := by
        field_simp [h₄₅.ne']
        <;> ring_nf
        <;> field_simp [h₄₅.ne']
        <;> linarith
      rw [h₅₁] at h₅₀
      linarith
    have h₄₈ : Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) < 7 / 2 := by
      by_contra h₄₉
      have h₅₀ : Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) ≥ 7 / 2 := by linarith
      have h₅₁ : (Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) - 1) ^ 2 * (2 * Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) - 7) ≥ 0 := by
        have h₅₂ : Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) ≥ 7 / 2 := h₅₀
        have h₅₃ : 2 * Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) - 7 ≥ 0 := by linarith
        have h₅₄ : (Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) - 1) ^ 2 ≥ 0 := by nlinarith [Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x), Real.sq_sqrt (by linarith : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x)]
        nlinarith [Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x), Real.sq_sqrt (by linarith : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x)]
      have h₅₂ : (Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) - 1) ^ 2 * (2 * Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) - 7) ≥ 0 := h₅₁
      have h₅₃ : 4 * x ^ 2 ≥ (2 * x + 9) * (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 := by
        have h₅₄ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) ^ 2 = 2 + 2 * x - 2 * Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) := by
          nlinarith [Real.sq_sqrt (by linarith : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x), Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x)]
        rw [h₅₄]
        have h₅₅ : (Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) - 1) ^ 2 = 2 + 2 * x - 2 * Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) := by
          nlinarith [Real.sq_sqrt (by linarith : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x), Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x)]
        have h₅₆ : 4 * x ^ 2 ≥ (2 * x + 9) * (2 + 2 * x - 2 * Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x)) := by
          nlinarith [Real.sq_sqrt (by linarith : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x), Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x),
            sq_nonneg (Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) - 1), sq_nonneg (Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) - 7 / 2)]
        nlinarith [Real.sq_sqrt (by linarith : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x), Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x)]
      linarith
    have h₄₉ : 1 + 2 * x < (7 / 2) ^ 2 := by
      have h₅₀ : Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) < 7 / 2 := h₄₈
      have h₅₁ : 0 ≤ Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x) := Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x)
      nlinarith [Real.sq_sqrt (by linarith : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x), Real.sqrt_nonneg (1 + 2 * x)]
    have h₅₀ : x < 45 / 8 := by
      nlinarith
    exact h₅₀
  
  exact ⟨h₃, h₄⟩
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem Analysis:**
We are given:
1. \\( 0 \\leq 1 + 2x \\),
2. \\( (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\neq 0 \\),
3. \\( \\frac{4x^2}{(1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2} < 2x + 9 \\).

We need to prove:
1. \\( -\\frac{1}{2} \\leq x \\),
2. \\( x < \\frac{45}{8} \\).

**Observations:**
1. The expression \\( 1 + 2x \\) under the square root must be non-negative, which is already given by \\( 0 \\leq 1 + 2x \\).
2. The denominator \\( (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\) is never zero because of \\( h₁ \\). This implies \\( 1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\neq 0 \\), i.e., \\( \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\neq 1 \\), or equivalently \\( 1 + 2x \\neq 1 \\), i.e., \\( x \\neq 0 \\).
   - However, \\( h₁ \\) is stronger than \\( x \\neq 0 \\), as it rules out the possibility that \\( 1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x} = 0 \\).
   - But we can deduce \\( x \\neq 0 \\) from \\( h₁ \\), because if \\( x = 0 \\), then \\( 1 + 2x = 1 \\), and \\( (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 = 0 \\), contradicting \\( h₁ \\).
   - But we do not need \\( x \\neq 0 \\) explicitly; we can work with \\( h₁ \\) directly.
3. The inequality \\( \\frac{4x^2}{(1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2} < 2x + 9 \\) is non-trivial. To simplify it, we can try to relate the denominator to the numerator or find bounds.

**Key Idea:**
First, note that \\( \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 0 \\) because \\( 1 + 2x \\geq 0 \\). We can rewrite the denominator as follows:
\\[ (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 = 1 - 2\\sqrt{1 + 2x} + (1 + 2x) = 2 + 2x - 2\\sqrt{1 + 2x} = 2(1 + x - \\sqrt{1 + 2x}). \\]
However, this does not immediately help. Instead, we can think about the range of \\( \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\).

But perhaps a better approach is to consider the expression \\( (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\). Since \\( \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 0 \\), we have:
\\[ (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\geq 0. \\]
But \\( h₁ \\) tells us that \\( (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\neq 0 \\), so \\( (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 > 0 \\). Therefore, the denominator is positive, and the inequality can be rewritten as:
\\[ 4x^2 < (2x + 9)(1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2. \\]

But expanding \\( (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\) gives:
\\[ (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 = 1 - 2\\sqrt{1 + 2x} + (1 + 2x) = 2 + 2x - 2\\sqrt{1 + 2x}. \\]
Thus, the inequality becomes:
\\[ 4x^2 < (2x + 9)(2 + 2x - 2\\sqrt{1 + 2x}). \\]

This seems complicated, but we can try to find bounds or make substitutions. Let’s set \\( y = \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 0 \\), so \\( y^2 = 1 + 2x \\), and \\( x = \\frac{y^2 - 1}{2} \\). The condition \\( 0 \\leq 1 + 2x \\) is automatically satisfied since \\( y \\geq 0 \\). The condition \\( (1 - y)^2 \\neq 0 \\) is equivalent to \\( y \\neq 1 \\), i.e., \\( x \\neq 0 \\).

The inequality becomes:
\\[ 4 \\left( \\frac{y^2 - 1}{2} \\right)^2 < \\left( 2 \\cdot \\frac{y^2 - 1}{2} + 9 \\right) (2 + 2 \\cdot \\frac{y^2 - 1}{2} - 2y). \\]
Simplify the left side:
\\[ 4 \\cdot \\frac{(y^2 - 1)^2}{4} = (y^2 - 1)^2. \\]
Simplify the right side:
\\[ (y^2 - 1 + 9) (2 + (y^2 - 1) - 2y) = (y^2 + 8)(y^2 - 2y + 1) = (y^2 + 8)(y - 1)^2. \\]
Thus, the inequality is:
\\[ (y^2 - 1)^2 < (y^2 + 8)(y - 1)^2. \\]

Expand both sides:
Left: \\( y^4 - 2y^2 + 1 \\),
Right: \\( y^4 - 2y^3 + y^2 + 8y^2 - 16y + 8 = y^4 - 2y^3 + 9y^2 - 16y + 8 \\).

Subtract the left side from both sides:
\\[ 0 < (y^4 - 2y^3 + 9y^2 - 16y + 8) - (y^4 - 2y^2 + 1) = -2y^3 + 11y^2 - 16y + 7. \\]
So, we have:
\\[ 2y^3 - 11y^2 + 16y - 7 < 0. \\]

Factor the polynomial \\( P(y) = 2y^3 - 11y^2 + 16y - 7 \\):
Try \\( y = 1 \\): \\( P(1) = 2 - 11 + 16 - 7 = 0 \\).
Factor out \\( (y - 1) \\):
\\[ P(y) = (y - 1)(2y^2 - 9y + 7). \\]
Further factor \\( 2y^2 - 9y + 7 \\):
Roots are \\( y = \\frac{9 \\pm \\sqrt{81 - 56}}{4} = \\frac{9 \\pm 5}{4} \\), so \\( y = \\frac{14}{4} = \\frac{7}{2} \\) and \\( y = 1 \\).
Thus:
\\[ P(y) = (y - 1)(y - 1)(2y - 7) = (y - 1)^2 (2y - 7). \\]
Therefore, the inequality becomes:
\\[ (y - 1)^2 (2y - 7) < 0. \\]
Since \\( (y - 1)^2 \\geq 0 \\) and \\( (y - 1)^2 = 0 \\) only when \\( y = 1 \\), and \\( h₁ \\) implies \\( y \\neq 1 \\), we have \\( (y - 1)^2 > 0 \\). Thus, the inequality simplifies to:
\\[ 2y - 7 < 0, \\]
i.e., \\( y < \\frac{7}{2} \\).

But \\( y = \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 0 \\), and \\( y < \\frac{7}{2} \\), so:
\\[ \\sqrt{1 + 2x} < \\frac{7}{2}. \\]
Square both sides (since both sides are non-negative):
\\[ 1 + 2x < \\left( \\frac{7}{2} \\right)^2 = \\frac{49}{4}, \\]
i.e.,
\\[ 2x < \\frac{49}{4} - 1 = \\frac{45}{4}, \\]
i.e.,
\\[ x < \\frac{45}{8}. \\]

This gives us the second part of the desired conclusion: \\( x < \\frac{45}{8} \\).

For the first part, \\( -\\frac{1}{2} \\leq x \\), we can use the given \\( h₀: 0 \\leq 1 + 2x \\), which directly gives \\( x \\geq -\\frac{1}{2} \\). 

However, we must ensure that all steps are valid. In particular, when we squared \\( \\sqrt{1 + 2x} < \\frac{7}{2} \\), we assumed \\( \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 0 \\), which is true because the square root is always non-negative. 

But we must also ensure that \\( 1 + 2x \\geq 0 \\), which is already given by \\( h₀ \\).

**Proof of \\( x < \\frac{45}{8} \\):**
1. We have \\( y = \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 0 \\), \\( y \\neq 1 \\), and \\( (y - 1)^2 (2y - 7) < 0 \\).
2. Since \\( (y - 1)^2 > 0 \\), we get \\( 2y - 7 < 0 \\), i.e., \\( y < \\frac{7}{2} \\).
3. Squaring gives \\( 1 + 2x < \\frac{49}{4} \\), so \\( x < \\frac{45}{8} \\).

**Proof of \\( -\\frac{1}{2} \\leq x \\):**
This is directly given by \\( h₀: 0 \\leq 1 + 2x \\), so \\( x \\geq -\\frac{1}{2} \\).

However, we must ensure that all steps are valid in the context of the problem. In particular, we need to ensure that \\( y \\neq 1 \\) is correctly handled. 

But \\( h₁ \\) states that \\( (1 - \\sqrt{1 + 2x})^2 \\neq 0 \\), which is equivalent to \\( y \\neq 1 \\), because \\( y = \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 0 \\), and \\( (1 - y)^2 \\neq 0 \\) iff \\( y \\neq 1 \\).

**Intermediate Steps:**
1. Define \\( y = \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 0 \\), so \\( x = \\frac{y^2 - 1}{2} \\).
2. Rewrite the inequality in terms of \\( y \\).
3. Simplify the inequality to \\( (y - 1)^2 (2y - 7) < 0 \\).
4. Use \\( (y - 1)^2 > 0 \\) (since \\( y \\neq 1 \\)) to deduce \\( y < \\frac{7}{2} \\).
5. Square to get \\( x < \\frac{45}{8} \\).
6. The lower bound \\( x \\geq -\\frac{1}{2} \\) is directly from \\( h₀ \\).

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Prove \\( x \\geq -\\frac{1}{2} \\):**
   - From \\( h₀: 0 \\leq 1 + 2x \\), divide both sides by 2 to get \\( x \\geq -\\frac{1}{2} \\).

2. **Prove \\( x < \\frac{45}{8} \\):**
   - Define \\( y = \\sqrt{1 + 2x} \\geq 0 \\). Note that \\( y \\neq 1 \\) because \\( h₁ \\) implies \\( (1 - y)^2 \\neq 0 \\).
   - Rewrite the inequality \\( \\frac{4x^2}{(1 - y)^2} < 2x + 9 \\) in terms of \\( y \\).
   - Simplify the inequality to \\( (y - 1)^2 (2y - 7) < 0 \\).
   - Since \\( (y - 1)^2 > 0 \\), deduce \\( 2y - 7 < 0 \\), i.e., \\( y < \\frac{7}{2} \\).
   - Square to get \\( 1 + 2x < \\frac{49}{4} \\), so \\( x < \\frac{45}{8} \\).

### Lean 4 Proof with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem imo_1960_p2
(x : ℝ)
(h₀ : 0 ≤ 1 + 2 * x)
(h₁ : (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2 * x))^2 ≠ 0)
(h₂ : (4 * x^2) / (1 - Real.sqrt (1 + 2*x))^2 < 2*x + 9) :
-(1 / 2) ≤ x ∧ x < 45 / 8 :=
  have h₃ : -(1 / 2 : ℝ) ≤ x := by sorry
  have h₄ : x < (45 / 8 : ℝ) := by sorry
  exact ⟨h₃, h₄⟩
```

theorem mathd_numbertheory_427
(a : ℕ)
(h₀ : a = (∑ k ∈ (Nat.divisors 500), k)) :
∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => Nat.Prime x) (Nat.divisors a), k = 25 :=
  have h₁ : a = 1092 := by
    rw [h₀]
    rw [show (∑ k in Nat.divisors 500, k) = 1092 by
      rw [Nat.divisors]
      -- Calculate the sum of the divisors of 500
      rfl
      <;> decide
    ]
    <;> rfl
  
  have h₂ : ∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => Nat.Prime x) (Nat.divisors a), k = 25 := by
    rw [h₁]
    rw [show (Nat.divisors 1092) = {1, 2, 3, 4, 6, 7, 12, 13, 14, 21, 26, 28, 39, 42, 52, 78, 84, 91, 156, 182, 273, 364, 546, 1092} by
      rfl
      <;> decide
    ]
    -- Filter the prime numbers in the set of divisors of 1092
    norm_num [Finset.sum_filter, Finset.sum_range_succ, Nat.div_eq_of_lt, Nat.Prime]
    <;> decide
  
  rw [h₂]
  <;> rfl
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem Breakdown:**
1. We are given a natural number `a` such that `a` is equal to the sum of the divisors of `500`.
2. We need to find the sum of all prime divisors of `a` (i.e., the sum of all prime numbers that divide `a`).

**Step 1: Find the sum of the divisors of 500.**
First, factorize `500`:
\\[ 500 = 2^2 \\times 5^3 \\]
The number of divisors of `500` is `(2 + 1)(3 + 1) = 12`. The sum of the divisors is:
\\[ \\sigma(500) = (1 + 2 + 2^2)(1 + 5 + 5^2 + 5^3) = 7 \\times 156 = 1092 \\]
Thus, `a = 1092`.

**Verification of the Sum of Divisors:**
The divisors of `500` are:
\\[ 1, 2, 4, 5, 10, 20, 25, 50, 100, 125, 250, 500 \\]
Summing them up:
\\[ 1 + 2 + 4 + 5 + 10 + 20 + 25 + 50 + 100 + 125 + 250 + 500 = 1092 \\]

**Step 2: Find the prime divisors of `a = 1092`.**
First, factorize `1092`:
\\[ 1092 = 2^2 \\times 3 \\times 7 \\times 13 \\]
The prime divisors of `1092` are `2`, `3`, `7`, and `13`. The sum of these primes is:
\\[ 2 + 3 + 7 + 13 = 25 \\]

**Step 3: Formal Verification in Lean.**
In Lean, we need to:
1. Compute `a = 1092`.
2. Find the prime divisors of `1092` (`2`, `3`, `7`, `13`).
3. Sum them to get `25`.

**Step 4: Verify Lean's `Nat.divisors` and `Finset.filter`.**
Lean's `Nat.divisors n` returns the multiset of positive divisors of `n`. The sum is straightforward. The `Finset.filter` will select only the primes, and the sum is `25`.

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Compute `a`:**
   - Calculate the sum of the divisors of `500` to get `a = 1092`.

2. **Factorize `a`:**
   - Factorize `1092` into its prime factors: `2² × 3 × 7 × 13`.

3. **Identify Prime Divisors:**
   - The distinct prime factors are `2`, `3`, `7`, and `13`.

4. **Sum the Primes:**
   - The sum is `2 + 3 + 7 + 13 = 25`.

5. **Verify Lean's Computation:**
   - Ensure that Lean correctly computes `a` and the sum of its prime divisors.

### Lean 4 Proof with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_numbertheory_427
(a : ℕ)
(h₀ : a = (∑ k ∈ (Nat.divisors 500), k)) :
∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => Nat.Prime x) (Nat.divisors a), k = 25 :=
  have h₁ : a = 1092 := by sorry
  have h₂ : ∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => Nat.Prime x) (Nat.divisors a), k = 25 := by sorry
  sorry
```

[[proof]]
name = "numbertheory_x5neqy2p4"
iteration = 22
time = 202
verified = false
proof = """
theorem numbertheory_x5neqy2p4
(x y : ℤ) :
x^5 ≠ y^2 + 4 :=
by
  have h_main : x^5 ≠ y^2 + 4 := by
    intro h
    have h₁ := h
    rw [← Int.emod_add_ediv x 2] at h₁
    rw [← Int.emod_add_ediv y 2] at h₁
    ring_nf at h₁
    have h₂ : x % 2 = 0 ∨ x % 2 = 1 := by omega
    have h₃ : y % 2 = 0 ∨ y % 2 = 1 := by omega
    rcases h₂ with (h₂ | h₂) <;> rcases h₃ with (h₃ | h₃) <;> simp [h₂, h₃, pow_succ, Int.mul_emod, Int.add_emod, Int.sub_emod, Int.emod_emod] at h₁
    <;>
    (try omega) <;>
    (try {
      have h₄ : x % 4 = 0 ∨ x % 4 = 1 ∨ x % 4 = 2 ∨ x % 4 = 3 := by omega
      have h₅ : y % 4 = 0 ∨ y % 4 = 1 ∨ y % 4 = 2 ∨ y % 4 = 3 := by omega
      rcases h₄ with (h₄ | h₄ | h₄ | h₄) <;> rcases h₅ with (h₅ | h₅ | h₅ | h₅) <;> simp [h₄, h₅, pow_succ, Int.mul_emod, Int.add_emod, Int.sub_emod, Int.emod_emod] at h₁ <;>
      (try omega) <;>
      (try {
        have h₆ : x % 5 = 0 ∨ x % 5 = 1 ∨ x % 5 = 2 ∨ x % 5 = 3 ∨ x % 5 = 4 := by omega
        have h₇ : y % 5 = 0 ∨ y % 5 = 1 ∨ y % 5 = 2 ∨ y % 5 = 3 ∨ y % 5 = 4 := by omega
        rcases h₆ with (h₆ | h₆ | h₆ | h₆ | h₆) <;> rcases h₇ with (h₇ | h₇ | h₇ | h₇ | h₇) <;> simp [h₆, h₇, pow_succ, Int.mul_emod, Int.add_emod, Int.sub_emod, Int.emod_emod] at h₁ <;>
        (try omega) <;>
        (try {
          omega
        })
      })
    }) <;>
    omega
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof

**Problem:** Prove that for all integers \\( x \\) and \\( y \\), \\( x^5 \\neq y^2 + 4 \\).

#### Key Observations:
1. The equation \\( x^5 = y^2 + 4 \\) can be rearranged to \\( y^2 = x^5 - 4 \\). This suggests that \\( x^5 - 4 \\) must be a perfect square.
2. We can analyze the possible values of \\( x \\) modulo small integers to derive contradictions or restrictions.
3. We can also consider the possible values of \\( y \\) modulo small integers to derive contradictions or restrictions.
4. The difference between \\( x^5 \\) and \\( y^2 \\) is \\( 4 \\), so we can consider the possible residues of \\( x^5 \\) and \\( y^2 \\) modulo small integers to find a contradiction.

#### Proof Sketch:
1. Consider the equation modulo 5:
   - \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{5} \\) by Fermat's Little Theorem (since \\( \\phi(5) = 4 \\) and \\( x^4 \\equiv 1 \\pmod{5} \\) when \\( x \\not\\equiv 0 \\pmod{5} \\), so \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{5} \\)).
   - \\( y^2 \\) modulo 5 can be \\( 0, 1, 4 \\) because squares modulo 5 are \\( 0^2 \\equiv 0 \\), \\( 1^2 \\equiv 1 \\), \\( 2^2 \\equiv 4 \\), \\( 3^2 \\equiv 4 \\), and \\( 4^2 \\equiv 1 \\).
   - The equation becomes \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\). So \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\).
   - The possible values of \\( y^2 \\pmod{5} \\) are \\( 0, 1, 4 \\), so \\( y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\) can be \\( 4, 0, 3 \\).
   - Thus, \\( x \\equiv 4, 0, 3 \\pmod{5} \\).
2. Consider the equation modulo 4:
   - \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{4} \\) because \\( x^5 - x = x(x^4 - 1) \\), and \\( x^4 \\equiv 1 \\pmod{4} \\) for \\( x \\) odd, and \\( x^4 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4} \\) for \\( x \\) even.
   - \\( y^2 \\pmod{4} \\) can be \\( 0 \\) or \\( 1 \\) because:
     - If \\( y \\) is even, \\( y^2 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4} \\).
     - If \\( y \\) is odd, \\( y^2 \\equiv 1 \\pmod{4} \\).
   - The equation becomes \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{4} \\), i.e., \\( x \\equiv y^2 \\pmod{4} \\).
   - So \\( x \\equiv y^2 \\pmod{4} \\).
3. Consider the equation modulo 3:
   - \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{3} \\) because \\( x^5 - x = x(x^4 - 1) \\), and \\( x^4 \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3} \\) for \\( x \\not\\equiv 0 \\pmod{3} \\), so \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{3} \\).
   - \\( y^2 \\pmod{3} \\) can be \\( 0 \\) or \\( 1 \\) because:
     - \\( 0^2 \\equiv 0 \\), \\( 1^2 \\equiv 1 \\), \\( 2^2 \\equiv 1 \\).
   - The equation becomes \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{3} \\), i.e., \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 1 \\pmod{3} \\).
   - So \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 1 \\pmod{3} \\).
4. Combining the results from modulo 5 and modulo 4:
   - We have \\( x \\equiv y^2 \\pmod{4} \\) and \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\).
   - The possible residues of \\( y^2 \\) modulo 4 are \\( 0 \\) and \\( 1 \\), so \\( x \\equiv 0 \\) or \\( 1 \\pmod{4} \\).
   - The possible residues of \\( y^2 \\) modulo 5 are \\( 0, 1, 4 \\), so \\( x \\equiv 4, 0, 3 \\pmod{5} \\).
   - We can check all combinations:
     - \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4} \\), \\( x \\equiv 4 \\pmod{5} \\): \\( x = 4 \\) is a candidate. But \\( 4^5 = 1024 \\) and \\( y^2 + 4 = 1024 \\) implies \\( y^2 = 1020 \\). But \\( 31^2 = 961 \\) and \\( 32^2 = 1024 \\), so \\( y^2 = 1020 \\) is not a perfect square.
     - \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4} \\), \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{5} \\): \\( x = 0 \\). Then \\( y^2 = -4 \\), which is impossible.
     - \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4} \\), \\( x \\equiv 3 \\pmod{5} \\): \\( x = 3 \\) is a candidate. Then \\( 3^5 = 243 \\), so \\( y^2 = 239 \\). But \\( 15^2 = 225 \\) and \\( 16^2 = 256 \\), so \\( y^2 = 239 \\) is not a perfect square.
     - \\( x \\equiv 1 \\pmod{4} \\), \\( x \\equiv 4 \\pmod{5} \\): \\( x = 9 \\) is a candidate. Then \\( 9^5 = 59049 \\), so \\( y^2 = 59045 \\). But \\( 243^2 = 59049 \\), so \\( y^2 = 59045 \\) is not a perfect square.
     - \\( x \\equiv 1 \\pmod{4} \\), \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{5} \\): \\( x = 5 \\). Then \\( 5^5 = 3125 \\), so \\( y^2 = 3121 \\). But \\( 55^2 = 3025 \\) and \\( 56^2 = 3136 \\), so \\( y^2 = 3121 \\) is not a perfect square.
     - \\( x \\equiv 1 \\pmod{4} \\), \\( x \\equiv 3 \\pmod{5} \\): \\( x = 13 \\). Then \\( 13^5 = 371293 \\), so \\( y^2 = 371289 \\). But \\( 609^2 = 370881 \\) and \\( 610^2 = 372100 \\), so \\( y^2 = 371289 \\) is not a perfect square.
   - We can continue this exhaustive search, but it is tedious. A better approach is to note that \\( x^5 - y^2 = 4 \\) implies \\( x^5 \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\), and \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{5} \\), so \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\). We can check all residues of \\( x \\) modulo 5 and see that none of them satisfy this equation for \\( y^2 \\) modulo 5.

However, we can instead use the fact that \\( x^5 - y^2 = 4 \\) is impossible because:
1. If \\( x \\) is even, \\( x^5 \\) is even, so \\( y^2 \\) is even, hence \\( y \\) is even. Let \\( x = 2k \\), \\( y = 2m \\). Then:
   \\[ (2k)^5 - (2m)^2 = 4 \\implies 32k^5 - 4m^2 = 4 \\implies 8k^5 - m^2 = 1 \\]
   But \\( 8k^5 \\) is even, so \\( m^2 \\) is odd. This is impossible because \\( m^2 \\) is a perfect square and cannot be odd (since \\( m \\) is integer). 

2. If \\( x \\) is odd, \\( x^5 \\) is odd, so \\( y^2 \\) is odd, hence \\( y \\) is odd. Let \\( x = 2k + 1 \\), \\( y = 2m + 1 \\). Then:
   \\[ (2k + 1)^5 - (2m + 1)^2 = 4 \\]
   Expanding \\( (2k + 1)^5 = 32k^5 + 80k^4 + 80k^3 + 40k^2 + 10k + 1 \\) and \\( (2m + 1)^2 = 4m^2 + 4m + 1 \\), we get:
   \\[ 32k^5 + 80k^4 + 80k^3 + 40k^2 + 10k + 1 - (4m^2 + 4m + 1) = 4 \\]
   Simplifying:
   \\[ 32k^5 + 80k^4 + 80k^3 + 40k^2 + 10k - 4m^2 - 4m - 1 = 4 \\]
   \\[ 32k^5 + 80k^4 + 80k^3 + 40k^2 + 10k - 4m^2 - 4m - 5 = 0 \\]
   Multiply by 2:
   \\[ 64k^5 + 160k^4 + 160k^3 + 80k^2 + 20k - 8m^2 - 8m - 10 = 0 \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[ 64k^5 + 160k^4 + 160k^3 + 80k^2 + 20k = 8m^2 + 8m + 10 \\]
   The left side is divisible by 8, so \\( 8m^2 + 8m + 10 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{8} \\), i.e., \\( 8m^2 + 8m + 10 \\equiv 2 \\pmod{8} \\), which is impossible. 

But this seems too complicated. A simpler approach is to consider the original equation modulo 4 and modulo 5:
1. \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{4} \\) and \\( y^2 \\equiv 0 \\) or \\( 1 \\pmod{4} \\), so \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{4} \\), i.e., \\( x \\equiv y^2 \\pmod{4} \\).
2. \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{5} \\) and \\( y^2 \\equiv 0, 1, 4 \\pmod{5} \\), so \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\).
   - If \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{5} \\), then \\( 0 \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\), i.e., \\( y^2 \\equiv 1 \\pmod{5} \\), which is possible (e.g., \\( y \\equiv 1 \\) or \\( 4 \\pmod{5} \\)).
   - If \\( x \\equiv 1 \\pmod{5} \\), then \\( 1 \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\), i.e., \\( y^2 \\equiv 2 \\pmod{5} \\), which is impossible since \\( y^2 \\equiv 0, 1, 4 \\pmod{5} \\).
   - If \\( x \\equiv 2 \\pmod{5} \\), then \\( 2 \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\), i.e., \\( y^2 \\equiv 3 \\pmod{5} \\), which is impossible.
   - If \\( x \\equiv 3 \\pmod{5} \\), then \\( 3 \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\), i.e., \\( y^2 \\equiv 4 \\pmod{5} \\), which is possible (e.g., \\( y \\equiv 2 \\) or \\( 3 \\pmod{5} \\)).
   - If \\( x \\equiv 4 \\pmod{5} \\), then \\( 4 \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{5} \\), i.e., \\( y^2 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{5} \\), which is possible (e.g., \\( y \\equiv 0 \\pmod{5} \\)).

But this is not immediately helpful. Instead, we can consider the equation modulo 3:
- \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{3} \\) and \\( y^2 \\equiv 0 \\) or \\( 1 \\pmod{3} \\), so \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 1 \\pmod{3} \\).
   - If \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3} \\), then \\( y^2 \\equiv 2 \\pmod{3} \\), which is impossible.
   - If \\( x \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3} \\), then \\( y^2 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3} \\), i.e., \\( y \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3} \\).
   - If \\( x \\equiv 2 \\pmod{3} \\), then \\( y^2 \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3} \\), i.e., \\( y \\equiv 1 \\) or \\( 2 \\pmod{3} \\).

This still seems complicated. A better approach is to consider the equation modulo 9:
- \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{9} \\) (since \\( \\phi(9) = 6 \\) and \\( x^6 \\equiv 1 \\pmod{9} \\) when \\( x \\not\\equiv 0 \\pmod{3} \\), and \\( x^5 \\equiv x \\pmod{9} \\) when \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3} \\)).
- \\( y^2 \\equiv 0, 1, 4, 7 \\pmod{9} \\) (since squares modulo 9 are \\( 0^2 \\equiv 0 \\), \\( 1^2 \\equiv 1 \\), \\( 2^2 \\equiv 4 \\), \\( 3^2 \\equiv 0 \\), \\( 4^2 \\equiv 7 \\), \\( 5^2 \\equiv 7 \\), \\( 6^2 \\equiv 0 \\), \\( 7^2 \\equiv 4 \\), \\( 8^2 \\equiv 1 \\)).
- The equation becomes \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{9} \\).
   - If \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3} \\), then \\( x \\equiv 0, 3, 6 \\pmod{9} \\). But \\( y^2 + 4 \\equiv 0, 1, 4, 7 \\pmod{9} \\), so \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{9} \\) is possible only if \\( x \\equiv 0 \\pmod{9} \\), \\( y^2 \\equiv 6 \\pmod{9} \\), but \\( y^2 \\equiv 6 \\pmod{9} \\) is impossible.
   - If \\( x \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3} \\), then \\( x \\equiv 1, 4, 7 \\pmod{9} \\). The equation becomes \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{9} \\). But \\( y^2 + 4 \\) can be \\( 4, 5, 8 \\pmod{9} \\). So \\( x \\equiv 4, 5, 8 \\pmod{9} \\). But \\( x \\equiv 1, 4, 7 \\pmod{9} \\), so no solution.
   - If \\( x \\equiv 2 \\pmod{3} \\), then \\( x \\equiv 2, 5, 8 \\pmod{9} \\). The equation becomes \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{9} \\). But \\( y^2 + 4 \\) can be \\( 5, 6, 8 \\pmod{9} \\). So \\( x \\equiv 5, 6, 8 \\pmod{9} \\). But \\( x \\equiv 2, 5, 8 \\pmod{9} \\), so no solution.

This seems to cover all cases, but it is tedious. A better approach is to note that \\( x^5 \\) is always congruent to \\( x \\) modulo 9, and \\( y^2 \\) is congruent to \\( 0, 1, 4, 7 \\pmod{9} \\). The equation \\( x \\equiv y^2 + 4 \\pmod{9} \\) can be checked for all possible residues of \\( x \\) and \\( y \\), but this is tedious. 

Instead, we can use the fact that \\( x^5 - y^2 = 4 \\) implies that \\( x^5 \\) is congruent to \\( y^2 + 4 \\) modulo 9. We can check that \\( x^5 \\) modulo 9 cycles through \\( 0, 1, 8 \\), and \\( y^2 + 4 \\) modulo 9 cycles through \\( 4, 5, 8 \\). The only overlap is \\( 8 \\), so we need \\( x^5 \\equiv 8 \\pmod{9} \\), i.e., \\( x \\equiv 2 \\pmod{3} \\), and \\( y^2 + 4 \\equiv 8 \\pmod{9} \\), i.e., \\( y^2 \\equiv 4 \\pmod{9} \\), i.e., \\( y \\equiv 2 \\) or \\( 7 \\pmod{9} \\). 

But this is still not straightforward. A simpler approach is to note that:
1. If \\( x \\) is even, \\( x^5 \\) is even, so \\( y^2 \\) is even, hence \\( y \\) is even. Let \\( x = 2k \\), \\( y = 2m \\). Then \\( (2k)^5 - (2m)^2 = 4 \\implies 32k^5 - 4m^2 = 4 \\implies 8k^5 - m^2 = 1 \\). But \\( 8k^5 \\) is even, so \\( m^2 \\) is odd, which is impossible since \\( m^2 \\) is a perfect square.
2. If \\( x \\) is odd, \\( x^5 \\) is odd, so \\( y^2 \\) is odd, hence \\( y \\) is odd. Let \\( x = 2k + 1 \\), \\( y = 2m + 1 \\). Then:
   \\[ (2k + 1)^5 - (2m + 1)^2 = 4 \\]
   Expanding:
   \\[ 32k^5 + 80k^4 + 80k^3 + 40k^2 + 10k + 1 - (4m^2 + 4m + 1) = 4 \\]
   \\[ 32k^5 + 80k^4 + 80k^3 + 40k^2 + 10k - 4m^2 - 4m - 1 = 4 \\]
   \\[ 32k^5 + 80k^4 + 80k^3 + 40k^2 + 10k - 4m^2 - 4m - 5 = 0 \\]
   Multiply by 2:
   \\[ 64k^5 + 160k^4 + 160k^3 + 80k^2 + 20k - 8m^2 - 8m - 10 = 0 \\]
   Rearrange:
   \\[ 64k^5 + 160k^4 + 160k^3 + 80k^2 + 20k = 8m^2 + 8m + 10 \\]
   The left side is divisible by 8, so \\( 8m^2 + 8m + 10 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{8} \\), i.e., \\( 8m^2 + 8m + 10 \\equiv 2 \\pmod{8} \\), which is impossible. 

Thus, the equation \\( x^5 = y^2 + 4 \\) has no integer solutions.

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Assume for contradiction** that \\( x^5 = y^2 + 4 \\) for integers \\( x \\) and \\( y \\).
2. **Case 1:** \\( x \\) is even.
   - Let \\( x = 2k \\), \\( y = 2m \\).
   - Substitute into the equation to get \\( 32k^5 - 4m^2 = 4 \\), i.e., \\( 8k^5 - m^2 = 1 \\).
   - Since \\( 8k^5 \\) is even, \\( m^2 \\) is odd, which is impossible since \\( m^2 \\) is a perfect square.
3. **Case 2:** \\( x \\) is odd.
   - Let \\( x = 2k + 1 \\), \\( y = 2m + 1 \\).
   - Substitute into the equation and expand to get a contradiction modulo 8.
4. **Conclusion:** The assumption leads to a contradiction in both cases, so no such integers \\( x \\) and \\( y \\) exist.

### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem Understanding:**
We are given a sequence of non-negative real numbers (indexed by `ℕ` but treated as `NNReal` in Lean) `a : ℕ → NNReal` such that the sum of squares of the first 100 terms (from `a₁` to `a₁₀₀`) is 1. We need to prove that the sum of the products of consecutive squares (from `a₁²a₂` to `a₉₉²a₁₀₀`) plus `a₁₀₀²a₁` is less than `12/25`.

However, there is a discrepancy in indexing:
- The sum `∑ x ∈ Finset.range 100, ((a (x + 1))^2)` corresponds to `a₁² + a₂² + ... + a₁₀₀²` because `Finset.range 100` is `{0, ..., 99}` and `x + 1` ranges from `1` to `100`.
- The sum to bound is `∑ x ∈ Finset.range 99, ((a (x + 1))^2 * a (x + 2)) + (a 100)^2 * a 1`, which is `a₁²a₂ + a₂²a₃ + ... + a₉₉²a₁₀₀ + a₁₀₀²a₁`.
- The upper bound is `12/25`, which is `0.48` as a decimal.

**Key Observations:**
1. All terms are non-negative (`NNReal`), so we can use inequalities freely.
2. The sum of squares is `1`, and we need to bound a weighted sum of products of these squares.
3. The expression `a₁²a₂ + ... + a₉₉²a₁₀₀ + a₁₀₀²a₁` is cyclic and can be bounded using inequalities like AM-GM or Cauchy-Schwarz.
4. The bound `12/25` is quite tight, so we need a careful approach.

**Approach:**
1. Notice that for each `i`, `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ (a_i² + a_{i+1}²)/2` by the AM-GM inequality (since `2ab ≤ a² + b²`).
   - However, this gives `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ (a_i² + a_{i+1}²)/2`, but we have `a_i²` and `a_{i+1}²` in the sum, so this might not directly help.
2. Alternatively, we can use the fact that `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i²` (since `a_{i+1} ≤ 1` if `a_i² ≤ 1`), but this is not necessarily true because `a_i` could be greater than `1` (but their squares sum to `1`, so no single `a_i` can be too large).
3. A better approach is to use the following inequality for each term `a_i² a_{i+1}`:
   - Since `a_i² + a_{i+1}² ≤ 1` (because the sum of all squares is `1`), we can write `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ (a_i² + a_{i+1}²) max(a_i, a_{i+1})`.
   - However, this seems complicated.
4. A simpler approach is to realize that the maximum of `a_i² a_{i+1}` under the constraint `∑ a_i² = 1` is `1/2` (achieved when `a_i = a_{i+1} = 1/√2`), but this is not directly useful because the sum has many terms.
5. Instead, we can use the following strategy:
   - The sum `S = a₁²a₂ + ... + a₉₉²a₁₀₀ + a₁₀₀²a₁` can be bounded by `S ≤ max(a₁, ..., a₁₀₀) * (a₁² + ... + a₁₀₀²) = max(a₁, ..., a₁₀₀)`.
   - But `max(a₁, ..., a₁₀₀) ≤ 1` because `∑ a_i² = 1` implies each `a_i² ≤ 1` and hence `a_i ≤ 1`.
   - This only gives `S ≤ 1`, which is worse than `12/25`.
   - This bound is too loose because we are ignoring the fact that `a_i` cannot all be large simultaneously.
6. A better bound can be obtained by noting that:
   - For each `i`, `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i²` (since `a_{i+1} ≤ 1` is not necessarily true, as `a_{i+1}` could be `> 1` if `a_i` is small).
   - This is not directly helpful unless we know more about the distribution of `a_i`.
7. Alternatively, we can use the rearrangement inequality or consider the maximum of `S` under the constraint `∑ a_i² = 1`. However, this is non-trivial.
8. A more promising approach is to use the fact that `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` (since `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is equivalent to `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²`, which is true because `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is equivalent to `0 ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}² - a_i² a_{i+1} = a_i²(1 - a_{i+1}) + a_{i+1}² ≥ 0` if `a_{i+1} ≤ 1`. But if `a_{i+1} > 1`, this is not necessarily true (e.g., `a_i = 0.1`, `a_{i+1} = 2` gives `a_i² a_{i+1} = 0.02` and `a_i² + a_{i+1}² = 0.01 + 4 = 4.01`, so `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` holds, but `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i²` does not).
   - Actually, the inequality `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is always true because `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is equivalent to `0 ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}² - a_i² a_{i+1}`, which is true because `a_i² + a_{i+1}² - a_i² a_{i+1} = a_i²(1 - a_{i+1}) + a_{i+1}² ≥ 0` if `a_{i+1} ≤ 1`, and if `a_{i+1} > 1`, then `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is equivalent to `a_i² ≤ a_i² / a_{i+1} + a_{i+1}`, which is true because `a_i² / a_{i+1} + a_{i+1} ≥ 2 a_i` (by AM-GM), but `a_i² ≤ 2 a_i` is not necessarily true (e.g., `a_i = 2`, `a_{i+1} = 2` gives `a_i² = 4` and `2 a_i = 4`, but `a_i² ≤ 2 a_i` is `4 ≤ 4`, which is true. Hmm, maybe it is always true?).
   - Actually, `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is equivalent to `a_i² (a_{i+1} - 1) ≤ a_{i+1}²`. If `a_{i+1} ≥ 1`, then `a_i² (a_{i+1} - 1) ≤ a_{i+1}²` is equivalent to `a_i² ≤ a_{i+1}² / (a_{i+1} - 1)`. But `a_{i+1}² / (a_{i+1} - 1) ≥ a_{i+1}` if `a_{i+1} > 1`, because `a_{i+1}² ≥ a_{i+1}(a_{i+1} - 1)` is equivalent to `a_{i+1} ≥ a_{i+1} - 1` or `0 ≥ -1`, which is true. So `a_{i+1}² / (a_{i+1} - 1) ≥ a_{i+1}`. But we need `a_i² ≤ a_{i+1}² / (a_{i+1} - 1)`, which is not necessarily true unless `a_i² ≤ a_{i+1}`. This is not guaranteed, so the inequality `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is not always true.
   - For example, take `a_i = 2`, `a_{i+1} = 2`. Then `a_i² a_{i+1} = 8` and `a_i² + a_{i+1}² = 4 + 4 = 8`, so `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is `8 ≤ 8`, which is true. Take `a_i = 3`, `a_{i+1} = 2`. Then `a_i² a_{i+1} = 18` and `a_i² + a_{i+1}² = 9 + 4 = 13`, so `18 ≤ 13` is false. Thus, the inequality `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is not always true.
   - Therefore, we cannot use this approach directly.
9. Another approach is to note that the sum `S = a₁²a₂ + ... + a₉₉²a₁₀₀ + a₁₀₀²a₁` is maximized when the `a_i` are as large as possible under the constraint `∑ a_i² = 1`. The maximum seems to be achieved when `a_i` are equal, i.e., `a_i = 1/√100 = 1/10` for all `i`. Then `S = 100 * (1/100 * 1/10) = 1/10 < 12/25`. But this is not the maximum, because if we set `a₁ = 1`, `a₂ = ... = a₁₀₀ = 0`, then `S = 0 < 12/25`. However, if we set `a₁ = a₂ = 1/√2` and the rest to `0`, then `S = (1/2)(1/√2) + (1/2)(0) + ... + (1/2)(0) = 1/(2√2) ≈ 0.353 < 12/25 ≈ 0.48`. This is still less than `12/25`.
   - But we need to find the actual maximum of `S` under `∑ a_i² = 1`.
   - Alternatively, we can try to find an upper bound for `S` that is `≤ 12/25`.
   - Note that `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i²` if `a_{i+1} ≤ 1`. But if `a_{i+1} > 1`, this is not necessarily true. However, if `a_{i+1} > 1`, then `a_i² ≤ 1 - a_{i+1}²` (because `∑ a_j² = 1` and `a_{i+1}² > 1` is impossible unless other terms are negative, but they are non-negative). Wait, no: if `a_{i+1} > 1`, then `a_{i+1}² > 1`, but `∑ a_j² = 1` implies `a_{i+1}² ≤ 1`, so `a_{i+1} ≤ 1`. Thus, `a_{i+1} ≤ 1` always holds because `a_{i+1}² ≤ ∑ a_j² = 1` implies `a_{i+1} ≤ 1`.
   - Therefore, `a_{i+1} ≤ 1` for all `i`, and hence `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i²`.
   - Thus, `S ≤ ∑ a_i² = 1`. But we need `S < 12/25`, which is not directly implied by this.
   - However, the problem is that `S` is not `≤ ∑ a_i²` because `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i²` is only true if `a_{i+1} ≤ 1`, which is true, but `S` is `∑ a_i² a_{i+1} + a₁₀₀² a₁`, and we have `a₁₀₀² a₁ ≤ a₁₀₀²`, so `S ≤ ∑ a_i² + a₁₀₀² = 1 + a₁₀₀²`. But `a₁₀₀² ≤ 1`, so `S ≤ 2`, which is not helpful.
   - The issue is that the bound `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i²` is too loose because it ignores the fact that `a_{i+1}` is small when `a_i` is large.
10. A better approach is to use the following observation:
    - The sum `S` can be written as `S = ∑_{i=1}^{99} a_i² a_{i+1} + a₁₀₀² a₁`.
    - Notice that `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ (a_i² + a_{i+1}²)/2` is not true (as shown earlier), but we can use `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ (a_i² + a_{i+1}²) max(a_i, a_{i+1})`, but this is not directly helpful.
    - Alternatively, note that `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²` is not always true, but we can find another bound.
    - A better idea is to use the fact that `a_i² a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}² - a_i a_{i+1}` is equivalent to `a_i² a_{i+1} + a_i a_{i+1} ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²`, which is equivalent to `a_i a_{i+1}(a_i + 1) ≤ a_i² + a_{i+1}²`. This is not obviously true, but perhaps we can find another bound.
11. Another approach is to use the following:
    - We know that `∑ a_i² = 1`, and we want to bound `S = ∑ a_i² a_{i+1} + a₁₀₀² a₁`.

proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_398
(a b c : ℝ)
(h₀ : 0 < a ∧ 0 < b ∧ 0 < c)
(h₁ : 9 * b = 20 * c)
(h₂ : 7 * a = 4 * b) :
63 * a = 80 * c :=
  have h_b : b = (20 / 9 : ℝ) * c := by
    have h₃ : 9 * b = 20 * c := h₁
    -- Solve for b in terms of c
    have h₄ : b = (20 / 9 : ℝ) * c := by
      -- Divide both sides by 9 to isolate b
      apply Eq.symm
      field_simp at h₃ ⊢
      <;> nlinarith
    exact h₄
  
  have h_a : a = (80 / 63 : ℝ) * c := by
    have h₃ : 7 * a = 4 * b := h₂
    have h₄ : b = (20 / 9 : ℝ) * c := h_b
    rw [h₄] at h₃
    -- Substitute b = (20 / 9) * c into the equation 7 * a = 4 * b
    have h₅ : 7 * a = 4 * ((20 / 9 : ℝ) * c) := by linarith
    -- Simplify the equation to solve for a
    have h₆ : 7 * a = (80 / 9 : ℝ) * c := by
      ring_nf at h₅ ⊢
      <;> nlinarith
    have h₇ : a = (80 / 63 : ℝ) * c := by
      -- Solve for a by dividing both sides by 7
      apply Eq.symm
      field_simp at h₆ ⊢
      <;> nlinarith
    exact h₇
  
  have h_main : 63 * a = 80 * c := by
    rw [h_a]
    <;> ring_nf
    <;> field_simp
    <;> nlinarith
  
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

We are given:
1. `a`, `b`, `c` are positive real numbers.
2. `9 * b = 20 * c`.
3. `7 * a = 4 * b`.

We need to prove that `63 * a = 80 * c`.

#### Step 1: Express `b` in terms of `c` using the second equation.
From `9 * b = 20 * c`, we can solve for `b`:
\\[ b = \\frac{20}{9} c \\]

#### Step 2: Express `a` in terms of `b` using the third equation.
From `7 * a = 4 * b`, we can solve for `a`:
\\[ a = \\frac{4}{7} b \\]

#### Step 3: Substitute `b` from Step 1 into the expression for `a` from Step 2.
Substitute `b = \\frac{20}{9} c` into `a = \\frac{4}{7} b`:
\\[ a = \\frac{4}{7} \\cdot \\frac{20}{9} c = \\frac{80}{63} c \\]

#### Step 4: Compute `63 * a` and simplify.
Multiply both sides by `63`:
\\[ 63 \\cdot a = 63 \\cdot \\frac{80}{63} c = 80 c \\]
Thus, `63 * a = 80 * c`, which is the desired result.

#### Verification:
We can also verify the result by eliminating `b` directly. From the given equations:
1. `9 * b = 20 * c` ⇒ `b = \\frac{20}{9} c`.
2. `7 * a = 4 * b = 4 * \\frac{20}{9} c = \\frac{80}{9} c` ⇒ `a = \\frac{80}{63} c`.
Then:
\\[ 63 \\cdot a = 63 \\cdot \\frac{80}{63} c = 80 c. \\]
This is consistent with our earlier calculation.

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Express `b` in terms of `c`:**
   - From `9 * b = 20 * c`, we get `b = (20/9) * c`.

2. **Express `a` in terms of `b`:**
   - From `7 * a = 4 * b`, we get `a = (4/7) * b`.

3. **Substitute `b` into the expression for `a`:**
   - Substitute `b = (20/9) * c` into `a = (4/7) * b` to get `a = (80/63) * c`.

4. **Compute `63 * a`:**
   - Multiply `a = (80/63) * c` by `63` to get `63 * a = 80 * c`.

### Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_algebra_398
(a b c : ℝ)
(h₀ : 0 < a ∧ 0 < b ∧ 0 < c)
(h₁ : 9 * b = 20 * c)
(h₂ : 7 * a = 4 * b) :
63 * a = 80 * c := by
  have h_b : b = (20 / 9 : ℝ) * c := by sorry
  have h_a : a = (80 / 63 : ℝ) * c := by sorry
  have h_main : 63 * a = 80 * c := by sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation:
1. `h_b`: We derive `b = (20 / 9) * c` from `9 * b = 20 * c` by dividing both sides by `9`.
2. `h_a`: We derive `a = (80 / 63) * c` by substituting `b = (20 / 9) * c` into `7 * a = 4 * b` and solving for `a`.
3. `h_main`: We substitute `a = (80 / 63) * c` into `63 * a` to get `80 * c`, which is the goal.

"""

theorem imo_1963_p5 :
Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7) = 1 / 2 :=
  by
  have h₀ : Real.sin (π / 7) > 0 := by
    apply Real.sin_pos_of_pos_of_lt_pi
    · linarith [Real.pi_pos, Real.pi_gt_three]
    · linarith [Real.pi_pos, Real.pi_gt_three]
  
  have h₁ : 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * (Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7)) = Real.sin (π / 7) := by
    have h₂ : 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (π / 7) = Real.sin (2 * π / 7) := by
      have h₃ : Real.sin (2 * π / 7) = 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (π / 7) := by
        have h₄ : Real.sin (2 * π / 7) = Real.sin (2 * (π / 7)) := by ring
        rw [h₄]
        have h₅ : Real.sin (2 * (π / 7)) = 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (π / 7) := by
          rw [Real.sin_two_mul]
          <;> ring
        rw [h₅]
        <;> ring
      linarith
    have h₃ : -2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (2 * π / 7) = - (Real.sin (3 * π / 7) - Real.sin (π / 7)) := by
      have h₄ : Real.sin (3 * π / 7) - Real.sin (π / 7) = 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (2 * π / 7) := by
        have h₅ : Real.sin (3 * π / 7) - Real.sin (π / 7) = 2 * Real.cos ((3 * π / 7 + π / 7) / 2) * Real.sin ((3 * π / 7 - π / 7) / 2) := by
          have h₆ : Real.sin (3 * π / 7) - Real.sin (π / 7) = 2 * Real.cos ((3 * π / 7 + π / 7) / 2) * Real.sin ((3 * π / 7 - π / 7) / 2) := by
            rw [Real.sin_sub_sin]
            <;> ring_nf
            <;> field_simp
            <;> ring_nf
          rw [h₆]
        rw [h₅]
        have h₇ : Real.cos ((3 * π / 7 + π / 7) / 2) = Real.cos (2 * π / 7) := by
          ring_nf
        have h₈ : Real.sin ((3 * π / 7 - π / 7) / 2) = Real.sin (π / 7) := by
          ring_nf
        rw [h₇, h₈]
        <;> ring
      linarith
    have h₄ : 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (3 * π / 7) = Real.sin (4 * π / 7) - Real.sin (2 * π / 7) := by
      have h₅ : Real.sin (4 * π / 7) - Real.sin (2 * π / 7) = 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (3 * π / 7) := by
        have h₆ : Real.sin (4 * π / 7) - Real.sin (2 * π / 7) = 2 * Real.cos ((4 * π / 7 + 2 * π / 7) / 2) * Real.sin ((4 * π / 7 - 2 * π / 7) / 2) := by
          have h₇ : Real.sin (4 * π / 7) - Real.sin (2 * π / 7) = 2 * Real.cos ((4 * π / 7 + 2 * π / 7) / 2) * Real.sin ((4 * π / 7 - 2 * π / 7) / 2) := by
            rw [Real.sin_sub_sin]
            <;> ring_nf
            <;> field_simp
            <;> ring_nf
          rw [h₇]
        rw [h₆]
        have h₈ : Real.cos ((4 * π / 7 + 2 * π / 7) / 2) = Real.cos (3 * π / 7) := by
          ring_nf
        have h₉ : Real.sin ((4 * π / 7 - 2 * π / 7) / 2) = Real.sin (π / 7) := by
          ring_nf
        rw [h₈, h₉]
        <;> ring
      linarith
    have h₅ : Real.sin (4 * π / 7) = Real.sin (3 * π / 7) := by
      have h₆ : Real.sin (4 * π / 7) = Real.sin (π - 3 * π / 7) := by
        ring_nf
      rw [h₆]
      have h₇ : Real.sin (π - 3 * π / 7) = Real.sin (3 * π / 7) := by
        rw [Real.sin_pi_sub]
      rw [h₇]
    have h₆ : 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * (Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7)) = Real.sin (π / 7) := by
      calc
        2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * (Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7)) =
            2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (π / 7) - 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * Real.cos (3 * π / 7) := by
          ring
        _ = Real.sin (2 * π / 7) - (Real.sin (3 * π / 7) - Real.sin (π / 7)) + (Real.sin (4 * π / 7) - Real.sin (2 * π / 7)) := by
          rw [h₂, h₃, h₄]
          <;> ring
        _ = Real.sin (π / 7) := by
          rw [h₅]
          <;> ring
          <;> linarith
        _ = Real.sin (π / 7) := by rfl
    exact h₆
  
  have h₂ : Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7) = 1 / 2 := by
    have h₃ : 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * (Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7)) = Real.sin (π / 7) := h₁
    have h₄ : Real.sin (π / 7) > 0 := h₀
    have h₅ : Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7) = 1 / 2 := by
      apply mul_left_cancel₀ (show (2 * Real.sin (π / 7)) ≠ 0 by linarith)
      nlinarith [Real.sin_le_one (π / 7), Real.cos_le_one (π / 7), Real.cos_le_one (2 * π / 7), Real.cos_le_one (3 * π / 7)]
    exact h₅
  
  apply h₂
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

We need to prove that:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) - \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]

#### Key Observations:
1. The angles are related as follows:
   - \\(\\frac{3\\pi}{7} = \\pi - \\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\), so \\(\\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = -\\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right)\\).
   - However, this doesn't seem immediately helpful, so we might consider a different approach.

2. The sum involves cosines of angles that are multiples of \\(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\). A useful identity for sums of cosines of angles in arithmetic progression is:
   \\[ \\sum_{k=0}^{n-1} \\cos\\left(a + kd\\right) = \\frac{\\sin\\left(\\frac{n d}{2}\\right) \\cos\\left(a + \\frac{(n-1)d}{2}\\right)}{\\sin\\left(\\frac{d}{2}\\right)}. \\]
   However, this might be overcomplicating things here.

3. A better approach is to use the identity for the sum of cosines of angles in a specific form:
   \\[ \\cos x + \\cos 3x + \\cos 5x = \\frac{\\sin 6x}{2 \\sin x}, \\]
   where \\(x = \\frac{\\pi}{7}\\). Here, \\(6x = \\frac{6\\pi}{7} = \\pi - x\\), so \\(\\sin 6x = \\sin x\\). Thus, the sum becomes:
   \\[ \\cos x + \\cos 3x + \\cos 5x = \\frac{\\sin x}{2 \\sin x} = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]
   But our original sum is \\(\\cos x - \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x\\), not \\(\\cos x + \\cos 3x + \\cos 5x\\). So we need to relate these sums.

4. Notice that:
   \\[ \\cos 2x = 2\\cos^2 x - 1, \\]
   and
   \\[ \\cos 4x = 2\\cos^2 2x - 1 = 2(2\\cos^2 x - 1)^2 - 1. \\]
   However, this seems complicated to work with.

5. A better approach is to use the identity:
   \\[ \\cos x - \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x = \\frac{1}{2}, \\]
   which can be derived using complex numbers or other trigonometric identities. Here, we will derive it using the product-to-sum identities and polynomial relations.

#### Step 1: Use the Identity for the Sum of Cosines
We will use the identity:
\\[ 2 \\sin x \\left( \\cos x - \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x \\right) = \\sin 2x - \\sin 2x + 2 \\sin 3x \\cos x. \\]
But this seems incorrect. Let's try a different approach.

#### Step 2: Use Complex Numbers (Optional)
Alternatively, we can use the roots of unity. The sum of the real parts of the 7th roots of unity (excluding 1) is zero. The roots are:
\\[ e^{2\\pi i k / 7} \\text{ for } k = 1, \\ldots, 6. \\]
The sum of their real parts is:
\\[ \\sum_{k=1}^6 \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi k}{7}\\right) = -1. \\]
However, we are dealing with \\(\\cos(\\pi k / 7)\\), not \\(\\cos(2\\pi k / 7)\\). This might not be directly helpful.

#### Step 3: Direct Calculation
Let’s compute \\(2 \\sin x (\\cos x - \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x)\\) using product-to-sum identities:
\\[
2 \\sin x \\cos x = \\sin 2x, \\\\
-2 \\sin x \\cos 2x = -(\\sin 3x - \\sin x), \\\\
2 \\sin x \\cos 3x = \\sin 4x - \\sin 2x.
\\]
Adding these gives:
\\[
2 \\sin x (\\cos x - \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x) = \\sin 2x - (\\sin 3x - \\sin x) + (\\sin 4x - \\sin 2x) = \\sin x - \\sin 3x + \\sin 4x.
\\]
Now, we need to simplify \\(\\sin x - \\sin 3x + \\sin 4x\\).

Notice that:
\\[
\\sin 4x = \\sin (7x - 3x) = \\sin \\pi \\cos 3x - \\cos \\pi \\sin 3x = 0 \\cdot \\cos 3x - (-1) \\sin 3x = \\sin 3x.
\\]
But \\(7x = \\pi\\), so:
\\[
\\sin 4x = \\sin (\\pi - 3x) = \\sin 3x.
\\]
Thus:
\\[
\\sin x - \\sin 3x + \\sin 4x = \\sin x - \\sin 3x + \\sin 3x = \\sin x.
\\]
Therefore:
\\[
2 \\sin x (\\cos x - \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x) = \\sin x.
\\]
Assuming \\(\\sin x \\neq 0\\), we can divide both sides by \\(2 \\sin x\\) to get:
\\[
\\cos x - \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x = \\frac{1}{2}.
\\]
Since \\(x = \\frac{\\pi}{7} \\neq 0\\), this is valid.

#### Step 4: Verification of \\(\\sin x \\neq 0\\)
Since \\(x = \\frac{\\pi}{7}\\) and \\(0 < x < \\pi\\), \\(\\sin x > 0\\).

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Compute \\(2 \\sin x (\\cos x - \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x)\\)** using product-to-sum identities:
   - \\(2 \\sin x \\cos x = \\sin 2x\\),
   - \\(-2 \\sin x \\cos 2x = -(\\sin 3x - \\sin x)\\),
   - \\(2 \\sin x \\cos 3x = \\sin 4x - \\sin 2x\\),
   - Sum them to get \\(\\sin x - \\sin 3x + \\sin 4x\\).

2. **Simplify \\(\\sin x - \\sin 3x + \\sin 4x\\)** using the angle addition formula:
   - Since \\(7x = \\pi\\), \\(\\sin 4x = \\sin (\\pi - 3x) = \\sin 3x\\),
   - Thus, the sum is \\(\\sin x\\).

3. **Conclude that \\(2 \\sin x (\\cos x - \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x) = \\sin x\\)**, and divide by \\(2 \\sin x\\) to get the result.

4. **Verify that \\(\\sin x \\neq 0\\)** because \\(x = \\frac{\\pi}{7}\\) is in \\((0, \\pi)\\).

### Lean 4 Proof with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem imo_1963_p5 :
Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7) = 1 / 2 :=
  by
  have h₀ : Real.sin (π / 7) > 0 := by sorry
  have h₁ : 2 * Real.sin (π / 7) * (Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7)) = Real.sin (π / 7) := by sorry
  have h₂ : Real.cos (π / 7) - Real.cos (2 * π / 7) + Real.cos (3 * π / 7) = 1 / 2 := by sorry
  sorry
```

proof = """
theorem mathd_numbertheory_430
(a b c : ℕ)
(h₀ : 1 ≤ a ∧ a ≤ 9)
(h₁ : 1 ≤ b ∧ b ≤ 9)
(h₂ : 1 ≤ c ∧ c ≤ 9)
(h₃ : a ≠ b)
(h₄ : a ≠ c)
(h₅ : b ≠ c)
(h₆ : a + b = c)
(h₇ : 10 * a + a - b = 2 * c)
(h₈ : c * b = 10 * a + a + a) :
a + b + c = 8 :=
  have h_b : b = 3 * a := by
    have h₉ : 10 * a + a - b = 2 * c := h₇
    have h₁₀ : c = a + b := by omega
    rw [h₁₀] at h₉
    have h₁₁ : 10 * a + a - b = 2 * (a + b) := by omega
    have h₁₂ : 11 * a - b = 2 * (a + b) := by omega
    have h₁₃ : 11 * a - b = 2 * a + 2 * b := by omega
    have h₁₄ : 11 * a - b = 2 * a + 2 * b := by omega
    have h₁₅ : 9 * a = 3 * b := by
      have h₁₆ : 11 * a - b = 2 * a + 2 * b := by omega
      have h₁₇ : 11 * a ≥ b := by
        omega
      have h₁₈ : 11 * a - b = 2 * a + 2 * b := by omega
      have h₁₉ : 9 * a = 3 * b := by
        omega
      exact h₁₉
    have h₂₀ : b = 3 * a := by
      omega
    exact h₂₀
  
  have h_c : c = 4 := by
    have h₉ : c * b = 10 * a + a + a := h₈
    have h₁₀ : b = 3 * a := h_b
    have h₁₁ : c = a + b := by omega
    rw [h₁₀] at h₉
    have h₁₂ : c * (3 * a) = 10 * a + a + a := by simpa [mul_add, add_mul, mul_comm, mul_left_comm, mul_assoc] using h₉
    have h₁₃ : c = a + 3 * a := by
      nlinarith
    have h₁₄ : c = 4 * a := by
      omega
    have h₁₅ : a ≤ 9 := by linarith
    have h₁₆ : a ≥ 1 := by linarith
    interval_cases a <;> norm_num at h₁₄ h₁₂ ⊢ <;>
    (try omega) <;>
    (try {
      simp_all [mul_comm, mul_assoc, mul_left_comm]
      <;> ring_nf at *
      <;> nlinarith
    }) <;>
    (try {
      omega
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      aesop
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      omega
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      aesop
    })
  
  have h_a : a = 1 := by
    have h₉ : a ≤ 9 := by linarith
    have h₁₀ : b ≤ 9 := by linarith
    have h₁₁ : c ≤ 9 := by linarith
    interval_cases a <;> norm_num [h_b, h_c] at h₆ h₇ h₈ h₃ h₄ h₅ ⊢ <;>
    (try omega) <;>
    (try {
      simp_all [mul_comm, mul_assoc, mul_left_comm]
      <;> ring_nf at *
      <;> nlinarith
    }) <;>
    (try {
      omega
    }) <;>
    (try {
      nlinarith
    })
    <;>
    (try {
      aesop
    })
  
  have h_final : a + b + c = 8 := by
    subst_vars
    <;> norm_num at * <;>
    (try omega) <;>
    (try nlinarith) <;>
    (try aesop) <;>
    (try nlinarith)
    <;>
    omega
  
  exact h_final

outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, let's carefully restate the problem and the hypotheses to ensure we understand them correctly.

We have three digits `a`, `b`, `c` (each between 1 and 9 inclusive) such that:
1. `a ≠ b`, `a ≠ c`, `b ≠ c`,
2. `a + b = c`,
3. `10 * a + a - b = 2 * c` (this can be simplified to `11 * a - b = 2 * c`),
4. `c * b = 10 * a + a + a` (this can be simplified to `c * b = 12 * a`).

We need to prove that `a + b + c = 8`.

#### Step 1: Simplify the Hypotheses

First, simplify the hypotheses:
1. From `a + b = c`, we can substitute `c = a + b` everywhere.
2. From `10 * a + a - b = 2 * c`, we get `11 * a - b = 2 * c`.
   - Substitute `c = a + b` into this to get `11 * a - b = 2 * (a + b) = 2 * a + 2 * b`.
   - Simplifying further: `11 * a - b = 2 * a + 2 * b` → `9 * a = 3 * b` → `3 * a = b`.
3. From `c * b = 12 * a`, substitute `c = a + b` and `b = 3 * a`:
   - `(a + b) * b = 12 * a` → `(a + 3 * a) * (3 * a) = 12 * a` → `(4 * a) * (3 * a) = 12 * a` → `12 * a² = 12 * a`.
   - Since `a ≥ 1`, we can divide both sides by `12 * a` to get `a = 1`.

But let's carefully verify this step-by-step.

#### Step 2: Derive `b = 3 * a` from the Simplified Hypotheses

From `11 * a - b = 2 * c` and `c = a + b`, substitute `c`:
`11 * a - b = 2 * (a + b) = 2 * a + 2 * b`.
Rearrange:
`11 * a - b = 2 * a + 2 * b` → `11 * a - 2 * a = 2 * b + b` → `9 * a = 3 * b` → `3 * a = b`.

Thus, `b = 3 * a`.

#### Step 3: Substitute `b = 3 * a` into `c * b = 12 * a`

Given `b = 3 * a`, we substitute into `c * b = 12 * a`:
`c * (3 * a) = 12 * a` → `3 * a * c = 12 * a`.
Since `a ≥ 1`, we can divide both sides by `a` to get `3 * c = 12` → `c = 4`.

But we must verify that `c = 4` is consistent with the other conditions.

#### Step 4: Find `a`, `b`, `c` with `c = a + b` and `c = 4`

Given `c = 4` and `c = a + b`, we get `a + b = 4`. But `b = 3 * a`, so `a + 3 * a = 4` → `4 * a = 4` → `a = 1`.
Then `b = 3 * 1 = 3` and `c = 4`.

Now, check the original conditions:
1. `a + b = 1 + 3 = 4 = c` ✔️.
2. `10 * a + a - b = 11 - 3 = 8` and `2 * c = 8` ✔️.
3. `c * b = 4 * 3 = 12` and `10 * a + a + a = 10 + 1 + 1 = 12` ✔️.
4. All digits are distinct: `a = 1`, `b = 3`, `c = 4` ✔️.

Thus, the unique solution is `a = 1`, `b = 3`, `c = 4`, and `a + b + c = 8`.

#### Step 5: Verify No Other Solutions Exist

We assumed that `a ≥ 1` and derived `a = 1` uniquely. For `a > 1`, the condition `3 * a = b` would require `b > 3`, but since `b ≤ 9`, `a ≤ 3`. However, `a ≥ 2` leads to contradictions:
1. If `a = 2`, then `b = 6`, `c = a + b = 8`.
   - Check `c * b = 8 * 6 = 48` and `12 * a = 24` → `48 ≠ 24` ✗.
2. If `a = 3`, then `b = 9`, `c = a + b = 12` → `c > 9` ✗.
Thus, the only solution is `a = 1`, `b = 3`, `c = 4`.

### Step 6: Abstract Plan

1. **Substitute `c = a + b` into the other equations**:
   - From `10 * a + a - b = 2 * c`, we get `11 * a - b = 2 * (a + b) = 2 * a + 2 * b`.
   - Simplify to `9 * a = 3 * b` → `b = 3 * a`.
2. **Substitute `b = 3 * a` into `c * b = 12 * a`**:
   - We get `(a + b) * b = 12 * a` → `(a + 3 * a) * (3 * a) = 12 * a` → `12 * a² = 12 * a`.
   - Since `a ≥ 1`, `a = 1` is the only solution.
3. **Find `b` and `c`**:
   - `a = 1`, `b = 3 * 1 = 3`, `c = a + b = 4`.
4. **Verify all conditions**:
   - Check that `a + b + c = 8` and all other constraints are satisfied.

### Lean 4 Proof Sketch with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_numbertheory_430
(a b c : ℕ)
(h₀ : 1 ≤ a ∧ a ≤ 9)
(h₁ : 1 ≤ b ∧ b ≤ 9)
(h₂ : 1 ≤ c ∧ c ≤ 9)
(h₃ : a ≠ b)
(h₄ : a ≠ c)
(h₅ : b ≠ c)
(h₆ : a + b = c)
(h₇ : 10 * a + a - b = 2 * c)
(h₈ : c * b = 10 * a + a + a) :
a + b + c = 8 := by
  have h_b : b = 3 * a := by sorry
  have h_c : c = 4 := by sorry
  have h_a : a = 1 := by sorry
  have h_final : a + b + c = 8 := by sorry
  exact h_final
``` 

### Explanation of `have` Statements:
1. `h_b : b = 3 * a`: This is derived from the simplification of the equation `11 * a - b = 2 * c` and the substitution `c = a + b`.
2. `h_c : c = 4`: This is obtained by substituting `b = 3 * a` into `c * b = 12 * a` and simplifying.
3. `h_a : a = 1`: This is the unique solution derived from the constraints `a ≥ 1`, `b ≤ 9`, and `c ≤ 9`.
4. `h_final : a + b + c = 8`: This is the final result, which follows from substituting `a = 1`, `b = 3`, and `c = 4`.

"""

name = "mathd_algebra_459"

time = 105

    { line = 81, column = 0, message = "unknown identifier 'exact'" },

proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_459
(a b c d : ℚ)
(h₀ : 3 * a = b + c + d)
(h₁ : 4 * b = a + c + d)
(h₂ : 2 * c = a + b + d)
(h₃ : 8 * a + 10 * b + 6 * c = 24) :
↑d.den + d.num = 28 :=
have h₄ : a = (5 : ℚ) / 4 * b := by
  have h₄₁ : 5 * b = 4 * a := by
    have h₄₂ : 4 * b - 3 * a = a - b := by
      linarith
    linarith
  have h₄₂ : a = (5 : ℚ) / 4 * b := by
    have h₄₃ : a = (5 : ℚ) / 4 * b := by
      apply Eq.symm
      -- Solve for a in terms of b using the equation 5b = 4a
      ring_nf at h₄₁ ⊢
      linarith
    exact h₄₃
  exact h₄₂
  
have h₅ : c = (5 : ℚ) / 3 * b := by
  have h₅₁ : 3 * c = 4 * a := by
    have h₅₂ : 2 * c - 3 * a = a - c := by
      linarith
    linarith
  have h₅₂ : c = (5 : ℚ) / 3 * b := by
    have h₅₃ : c = (5 : ℚ) / 3 * b := by
      -- Substitute a = (5/4)b into 3c = 4a to find c in terms of b
      have h₅₄ : a = (5 : ℚ) / 4 * b := h₄
      rw [h₅₄] at h₅₁
      ring_nf at h₅₁ ⊢
      linarith
    exact h₅₃
  exact h₅₂
  
have h₆ : d = (13 : ℚ) / 12 * b := by
  have h₆₁ : d = (13 : ℚ) / 12 * b := by
    have h₆₂ : 3 * a = b + c + d := h₀
    rw [h₄, h₅] at h₆₂
    ring_nf at h₆₂ ⊢
    linarith
  exact h₆₁
  
have h₇ : b = (4 : ℚ) / 5 := by
  have h₇₁ : 8 * a + 10 * b + 6 * c = 24 := h₃
  rw [h₄, h₅] at h₇₁
  ring_nf at h₇₁ ⊢
  linarith
  
have h₈ : d = (13 : ℚ) / 15 := by
  rw [h₆, h₇]
  <;> norm_num
  <;> field_simp
  <;> ring_nf
  <;> norm_num
  
have h₉ : ↑d.den + d.num = 28 := by
  rw [h₈]
  -- Convert the rational number to its numerator and denominator
  have h₉₁ : ((13 : ℚ) / 15 : ℚ) = (13 : ℚ) / 15 := by norm_num
  rw [h₉₁]
  -- Use the fact that the rational number is in its simplest form
  have h₉₂ : ((13 : ℚ) / 15 : ℚ) = (13 : ℤ) / (15 : ℤ) := by norm_num
  rw [h₉₂]
  -- Simplify the expression to get the final result
  norm_cast
  <;> simp [Rat.num_div_den]
  <;> norm_num
  <;> rfl
  
exact h₉
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof

**Problem Analysis:**
We have four rational numbers \\( a, b, c, d \\) and four equations:
1. \\( 3a = b + c + d \\)
2. \\( 4b = a + c + d \\)
3. \\( 2c = a + b + d \\)
4. \\( 8a + 10b + 6c = 24 \\)

We need to prove that the denominator of \\( d \\) (when \\( d \\) is written in lowest terms) plus the numerator of \\( d \\) is 28.

**Approach:**
First, we can solve for \\( a, b, c \\) in terms of \\( d \\) using the first three equations. Then, substitute these expressions into the fourth equation to find a relationship involving \\( d \\). Finally, we can determine \\( d \\) explicitly and compute its numerator and denominator in lowest terms.

**Step 1: Express \\( a, b, c \\) in terms of \\( d \\).**

From the first equation:
\\[ 3a = b + c + d \\quad \\text{(1)} \\]

From the second equation:
\\[ 4b = a + c + d \\quad \\text{(2)} \\]

From the third equation:
\\[ 2c = a + b + d \\quad \\text{(3)} \\]

**Step 2: Subtract (1) from (2).**
Subtracting (1) from (2) gives:
\\[ 4b - 3a = (a + c + d) - (b + c + d) = a - b \\]
\\[ 4b - 3a = a - b \\]
\\[ 5b = 4a \\]
\\[ a = \\frac{5}{4}b \\quad \\text{(4)} \\]

**Step 3: Subtract (1) from (3).**
Subtracting (1) from (3) gives:
\\[ 2c - 3a = (a + b + d) - (b + c + d) = a - c \\]
\\[ 2c - 3a = a - c \\]
\\[ 3c = 4a \\]
\\[ a = \\frac{3}{4}c \\quad \\text{(5)} \\]

**Step 4: Equate (4) and (5).**
From (4) and (5), we have:
\\[ \\frac{5}{4}b = \\frac{3}{4}c \\]
\\[ 5b = 3c \\]
\\[ c = \\frac{5}{3}b \\quad \\text{(6)} \\]

**Step 5: Express everything in terms of \\( b \\).**
Now, we can write \\( a \\) and \\( c \\) in terms of \\( b \\):
\\[ a = \\frac{5}{4}b \\]
\\[ c = \\frac{5}{3}b \\]

Substitute these into (1):
\\[ 3 \\cdot \\frac{5}{4}b = b + \\frac{5}{3}b + d \\]
\\[ \\frac{15}{4}b = b + \\frac{5}{3}b + d \\]
\\[ d = \\frac{15}{4}b - b - \\frac{5}{3}b \\]
\\[ d = \\left( \\frac{15}{4} - 1 - \\frac{5}{3} \\right)b \\]
\\[ d = \\left( \\frac{15}{4} - \\frac{4}{4} - \\frac{5}{3} \\right)b \\]
\\[ d = \\left( \\frac{11}{4} - \\frac{5}{3} \\right)b \\]
\\[ d = \\left( \\frac{33}{12} - \\frac{20}{12} \\right)b \\]
\\[ d = \\frac{13}{12}b \\quad \\text{(7)} \\]

**Step 6: Substitute into the fourth equation.**
The fourth equation is:
\\[ 8a + 10b + 6c = 24 \\]
Substitute \\( a = \\frac{5}{4}b \\) and \\( c = \\frac{5}{3}b \\):
\\[ 8 \\cdot \\frac{5}{4}b + 10b + 6 \\cdot \\frac{5}{3}b = 24 \\]
\\[ 10b + 10b + 10b = 24 \\]
\\[ 30b = 24 \\]
\\[ b = \\frac{24}{30} = \\frac{4}{5} \\]

**Step 7: Find \\( d \\).**
Using (7):
\\[ d = \\frac{13}{12} \\cdot \\frac{4}{5} = \\frac{52}{60} = \\frac{13}{15} \\]

**Step 8: Compute \\( \\text{den}(d) + \\text{num}(d) \\).**
The rational number \\( d = \\frac{13}{15} \\) is already in lowest terms, so:
\\[ \\text{den}(d) = 15 \\]
\\[ \\text{num}(d) = 13 \\]
Thus:
\\[ \\text{den}(d) + \\text{num}(d) = 15 + 13 = 28 \\]

**Verification:**
Check that all original equations are satisfied with \\( a = \\frac{5}{4} \\cdot \\frac{4}{5} = 1 \\), \\( b = \\frac{4}{5} \\), \\( c = \\frac{5}{3} \\cdot \\frac{4}{5} = \\frac{4}{3} \\), and \\( d = \\frac{13}{15} \\):
1. \\( 3a = 3 \\cdot 1 = 3 \\) and \\( b + c + d = \\frac{4}{5} + \\frac{4}{3} + \\frac{13}{15} = \\frac{12}{15} + \\frac{20}{15} + \\frac{13}{15} = \\frac{45}{15} = 3 \\). Correct.
2. \\( 4b = 4 \\cdot \\frac{4}{5} = \\frac{16}{5} \\) and \\( a + c + d = 1 + \\frac{4}{3} + \\frac{13}{15} = \\frac{15}{15} + \\frac{20}{15} + \\frac{13}{15} = \\frac{48}{15} = \\frac{16}{5} \\). Correct.
3. \\( 2c = 2 \\cdot \\frac{4}{3} = \\frac{8}{3} \\) and \\( a + b + d = 1 + \\frac{4}{5} + \\frac{13}{15} = \\frac{15}{15} + \\frac{12}{15} + \\frac{13}{15} = \\frac{40}{15} = \\frac{8}{3} \\). Correct.
4. \\( 8a + 10b + 6c = 8 \\cdot 1 + 10 \\cdot \\frac{4}{5} + 6 \\cdot \\frac{4}{3} = 8 + 8 + 8 = 24 \\). Correct.

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Express \\( a \\) in terms of \\( b \\):**
   - Subtract the first equation from the second to get \\( a = \\frac{5}{4}b \\).

2. **Express \\( c \\) in terms of \\( b \\):**
   - Subtract the first equation from the third to get \\( a = \\frac{3}{4}c \\).
   - Equate the two expressions for \\( a \\) to get \\( c = \\frac{5}{3}b \\).

3. **Express \\( d \\) in terms of \\( b \\):**
   - Substitute \\( a \\) and \\( c \\) into the first equation to get \\( d = \\frac{13}{12}b \\).

4. **Find \\( b \\):**
   - Substitute \\( a \\) and \\( c \\) into the fourth equation to get \\( b = \\frac{4}{5} \\).

5. **Find \\( d \\):**
   - Substitute \\( b \\) into the expression for \\( d \\) to get \\( d = \\frac{13}{15} \\).

6. **Compute \\( \\text{den}(d) + \\text{num}(d) \\):**
   - Since \\( d = \\frac{13}{15} \\) is in lowest terms, the sum is \\( 15 + 13 = 28 \\).

### Lean 4 Proof with `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem mathd_algebra_459
(a b c d : ℚ)
(h₀ : 3 * a = b + c + d)
(h₁ : 4 * b = a + c + d)
(h₂ : 2 * c = a + b + d)
(h₃ : 8 * a + 10 * b + 6 * c = 24) :
↑d.den + d.num = 28 :=
have h₄ : a = (5 : ℚ) / 4 * b := by sorry
have h₅ : c = (5 : ℚ) / 3 * b := by sorry
have h₆ : d = (13 : ℚ) / 12 * b := by sorry
have h₇ : b = (4 : ℚ) / 5 := by sorry
have h₈ : d = (13 : ℚ) / 15 := by sorry
have h₉ : ↑d.den + d.num = 28 := by sorry
sorry
```

"""

[[proof]]
name = "induction_12dvd4expnp1p20"
iteration = 28
time = 62
verified = false
feedback = [
    { line = 22, column = 2, message = "unknown identifier 'exact'" },
]
proof = """
theorem induction_12dvd4expnp1p20
(n : ℕ) :
12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20 :=
  have h_main : 12 ∣ 4^(n+1) + 20 := by
    apply Nat.dvd_of_mod_eq_zero
    have h : 4 ^ (n + 1) % 12 = 4 := by
      induction n with
      | zero => norm_num
      | succ n ih =>
        simp [pow_succ, Nat.mul_mod, Nat.pow_mod, ih]
        <;> norm_num <;> omega
    omega
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem:** Prove that \\(12 \\mid 4^{n+1} + 20\\) for all natural numbers \\(n\\).

**Approach:**
To prove that \\(12 \\mid 4^{n+1} + 20\\), we can use modular arithmetic. Specifically, we will show that \\(4^{n+1} + 20 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{12}\\) for all \\(n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\). 

First, observe that \\(12 = 4 \\times 3\\), so it suffices to show that \\(4^{n+1} + 20 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4}\\) and \\(4^{n+1} + 20 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3}\\), because \\(\\gcd(4, 3) = 1\\). 

1. **Modulo 4:**
   - \\(4^{n+1} \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4}\\) for \\(n \\geq 0\\) (since \\(4 \\mid 4^{n+1}\\)).
   - \\(20 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4}\\).
   - Therefore, \\(4^{n+1} + 20 \\equiv 0 + 0 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4}\\).

2. **Modulo 3:**
   - We compute \\(4^{n+1} \\pmod{3}\\). Note that \\(4 \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3}\\), so \\(4^{n+1} \\equiv 1^{n+1} \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3}\\).
   - \\(20 \\equiv 2 \\pmod{3}\\) (since \\(20 = 3 \\times 6 + 2\\)).
   - Therefore, \\(4^{n+1} + 20 \\equiv 1 + 2 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3}\\).

Since \\(4^{n+1} + 20 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{4}\\) and \\(4^{n+1} + 20 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3}\\), and \\(\\gcd(4, 3) = 1\\), it follows that \\(4^{n+1} + 20 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{12}\\).

**Simpler Approach:**
Alternatively, we can directly compute \\(4^{n+1} + 20\\) modulo \\(12\\) for small values of \\(n\\) and observe a pattern:
- For \\(n = 0\\): \\(4^{1} + 20 = 24 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{12}\\).
- For \\(n = 1\\): \\(4^{2} + 20 = 16 + 20 = 36 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{12}\\).
- For \\(n = 2\\): \\(4^{3} + 20 = 64 + 20 = 84 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{12}\\).
- For \\(n = 3\\): \\(4^{4} + 20 = 256 + 20 = 276 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{12}\\) (since \\(276 = 12 \\times 23\\)).

We can generalize this by observing that \\(4^{n+1} + 20 = 4(4^n + 5)\\), and since \\(4^n \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3}\\) for all \\(n \\geq 0\\), we have \\(4^n + 5 \\equiv 1 + 2 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3}\\). Thus, \\(4(4^n + 5)\\) is divisible by \\(3\\) and \\(4\\), hence by \\(12\\).

But we can also directly use the fact that \\(4^{n+1} + 20 = 4(4^n + 5)\\), and since \\(4^n \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3}\\) for all \\(n \\geq 0\\), we have \\(4^n + 5 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3}\\). Therefore, \\(4(4^n + 5)\\) is divisible by \\(3\\). Moreover, \\(4(4^n + 5)\\) is clearly divisible by \\(4\\). Hence, it is divisible by \\(\\text{lcm}(4, 3) = 12\\).

But we can also use induction. The base case \\(n = 0\\) is \\(4^1 + 20 = 24 = 12 \\times 2\\), which is divisible by \\(12\\). For the inductive step, assume \\(12 \\mid 4^{k+1} + 20\\). Then:
\\[ 4^{(k+1)+1} + 20 = 4 \\cdot 4^{k+1} + 20 = 4(4^{k+1} + 5). \\]
By the inductive hypothesis, \\(4^{k+1} + 20 = 12m\\) for some integer \\(m\\), so \\(4^{k+1} = 12m - 20\\). Substitute this into the expression for \\(4^{(k+1)+1} + 20\\):
\\[ 4^{(k+1)+1} + 20 = 4(12m - 20 + 5) = 4(12m - 15) = 48m - 60 = 12(4m - 5). \\]
This is clearly divisible by \\(12\\). 

Alternatively, we can directly observe that \\(4^{n+1} + 20 = 4^{n+1} + 16 + 4 = 4(4^n + 4) + 4\\). This seems less straightforward, so the inductive approach is better.

But the simplest approach is to note that \\(4^{n+1} + 20 = 4(4^n + 5)\\), and \\(4^n \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3}\\), so \\(4^n + 5 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3}\\). Hence, \\(4(4^n + 5)\\) is divisible by \\(3\\), and since \\(4(4^n + 5)\\) is clearly divisible by \\(4\\), it is divisible by \\(12\\).

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Factor the Expression:**
   - Rewrite \\(4^{n+1} + 20\\) as \\(4(4^n + 5)\\).

2. **Prove Divisibility by 3:**
   - Show that \\(4^n \\equiv 1 \\pmod{3}\\) for all \\(n \\geq 0\\).
   - Then, \\(4^n + 5 \\equiv 1 + 2 \\equiv 0 \\pmod{3}\\), so \\(3 \\mid 4^n + 5\\).
   - Therefore, \\(3 \\mid 4(4^n + 5)\\).

3. **Prove Divisibility by 4:**
   - \\(4^{n+1} + 20 = 4(4^n + 5)\\) is clearly divisible by \\(4\\).

4. **Combine the Results:**
   - Since \\(4(4^n + 5)\\) is divisible by both \\(3\\) and \\(4\\), and \\(\\gcd(3, 4) = 1\\), it is divisible by \\(12\\).

theorem mathd_numbertheory_559
(x y : ℕ)
(h₀ : x % 3 = 2)
(h₁ : y % 5 = 4)
(h₂ : x % 10 = y % 10) :
14 ≤ x :=
by
  have h₃ : x % 5 = 4 := by
    have h₃₁ : x % 5 = y % 5 := by
      have h₃₂ : x % 10 = y % 10 := h₂
      have h₃₃ : x % 5 = y % 5 := by
        omega
      exact h₃₃
    have h₃₄ : y % 5 = 4 := h₁
    omega
  
  have h₄ : x % 15 = 14 := by
    have h₄₁ : x % 15 = 14 := by
      have h₄₂ : x % 3 = 2 := h₀
      have h₄₃ : x % 5 = 4 := h₃
      have h₄₄ : x % 15 = 14 := by
        -- Use the Chinese Remainder Theorem to solve the system of congruences
        have h₄₅ : x % 15 = 14 := by
          -- Check all possible residues modulo 15
          have h₄₆ : x % 15 = 0 ∨ x % 15 = 1 ∨ x % 15 = 2 ∨ x % 15 = 3 ∨ x % 15 = 4 ∨ x % 15 = 5 ∨ x % 15 = 6 ∨ x % 15 = 7 ∨ x % 15 = 8 ∨ x % 15 = 9 ∨ x % 15 = 10 ∨ x % 15 = 11 ∨ x % 15 = 12 ∨ x % 15 = 13 ∨ x % 15 = 14 := by
            omega
          rcases h₄₆ with (h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆ | h₄₆) <;>
            (try omega) <;>
            (try {
              simp [h₄₆, Nat.add_mod, Nat.mul_mod, Nat.mod_mod, Nat.mod_eq_of_lt] at h₄₂ h₄₃ ⊢ <;>
              omega
            })
        exact h₄₅
      exact h₄₄
    exact h₄₁
  
  have h₅ : 14 ≤ x := by
    by_contra h
    -- Assume for contradiction that x < 14
    have h₅₁ : x < 14 := by omega
    -- Since x is a natural number and x < 14, we can check each possible value of x
    have h₅₂ : x ≤ 13 := by omega
    -- Use interval_cases to check each possible value of x
    interval_cases x <;> norm_num at h₀ h₃ h₄ ⊢ <;> omega
  
  exact h₅
